beginner

The Necessity of Divine Prohibition of Evil
Al-Nasafi argues that God must forbid evil to avoid permission of ugliness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi argues that God must forbid evil acts to avoid implying foolishness in His actions.

Core Idea
God's prohibition of evil is not arbitrary; it is required by wisdom.

This passage tackles a key question: If God is all-powerful, why does He command good and forbid evil? Could He simply allow everything? Al-Nasafi argues that not forbidding evil would be tantamount to permitting it, which is incompatible with divine wisdom. We will explore his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Nasafi uses a very compressed argument style.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the key conditional: if God did not forbid ugly acts, then that is permission of evil. And permission of evil is foolish.
3
Examine the supporting argument
The passage then describes what would happen if there were no obligation or punishment: it would give free rein to the soul commanding evil and the beastly soul, leading to chaos. This shows that prohibition is wise.
Note the vivid description of consequences.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends by stating that whoever thinks prevention of foolishness is foolish has inverted the story. Consider how this critique applies to those who deny the necessity of divine commands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكذا في أفعال الخلق ما هو قبيح، فلو لم ينه عنه الصانع لكان ذلك إباحة له إذ الإباحة هي زوال الحظر والأمر عن محتمل التكليف، وإباحة القبيح سفه. ومما يؤيد هذا أن في القول بإزالة التكليف والثواب والعقاب إعطاء الأمان للنفس الأمارة بالسوء عن عواقب المطالبات، وإغراء للنفس السبعية التي من دأبها القهر والغلبة على العبث والفساد والنهب والإغارة، وسفك الدماء وتخريب البلاد وحصول التفاني وهذا كله سفه وورود المنع عن ذلك كله حكمة، فمن ظن أن المنع عن سفه والإطلاق له حكمة فقد قلب القصة.
Similarly, among the actions of creatures there is what is ugly. For if the Maker did not forbid it, that would be permission of it, since permission is the removal of prohibition and command from one capable of obligation, and permission of the ugly is foolish. And among what supports this is that in the saying of removing obligation and reward and punishment is giving security to the soul commanding evil from the consequences of claims, and incitement for the beastly soul whose habit is domination and victory over frivolity, corruption, plunder, aggression, bloodshed, destruction of lands, and mutual annihilation. All of this is foolish, and the occurrence of prohibition against all that is wisdom. So whoever thinks that prevention of foolishness is foolishness and that giving free rein to it is wisdom has inverted the story.
Source pages p. 692

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the necessity of divine prohibition of evil. It begins by stating a general principle: among the actions of creatures, there is what is ugly (qabih). Then it argues that if the Creator did not forbid such an ugly act, that would constitute permission (ibaha) of it. Permission is defined as the removal of prohibition and command from one who is capable of being obligated (mukallaf). Permitting what is ugly is foolish (sufh), implying that it is not befitting of God's wisdom. The author then supports this by describing the consequences of removing taklif (obligation) and reward/punishment: it would give free rein to the soul that commands evil and incite the beastly soul, leading to chaos, corruption, bloodshed, and destruction. All of these are foolish, and preventing them is wisdom. Therefore, the one who thinks that preventing foolishness is foolishness and that allowing it is wisdom has completely reversed the truth.

Key Terms
قبيح
إباحة
سفه
تكليف
نفس أمارة بالسوء
نفس سبعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's commands are arbitrary and not based on rational considerations.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that not forbidding evil would be foolish, so divine prohibition is rooted in wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what would it mean if God did not forbid evil acts?
A) It would be wisdom.
B) It would be permission of evil, which is foolish.
C) It would be obligatory for God to allow them.
D) It would be neutral for God.

Key Takeaway
God's prohibition of evil is a necessary consequence of His wisdom; not forbidding it would be irrational and lead to chaos.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every command of God is rooted in wisdom, even when you do not understand it? What specific prohibition do you struggle to accept as wise? How can you change your heart about it starting today?
?
If God had not forbidden evil and left you entirely to your own desires, which ugly actions would your soul command? Honestly, what would your life become? Let that sink in, then thank Him for His prohibitions with a specific commitment to obey one command you've been neglecting.
?
The passage says stopping foolishness is wisdom. When have you resented God's boundaries as restrictive rather than protective? Will you today choose to see His limits as mercy, and let that reshape your obedience?

Denying Miraculous Change Attributes Weakness to God
Argument that denying miraculous change implies attributing impotence to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying God's ability to alter natural properties logically leads to attributing impotence to Him and the refutation of this view.

Core Idea
Denying God's power to change nature is denying His absolute power.

The naturalists may accept that God created the world, but they often deny that He can intervene miraculously. This passage from Imam al-Nasafi argues that such denial logically leads to describing God as impotent and His actions as compelled. Let's see how the author builds this case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the flow from common ground to the conclusion.
The author begins by establishing that both parties agree God created all things.
2
Identify the common ground
The author points out that the opponents agree that everything in the world is created by God, including fire and its properties. This shared premise is key.
3
Understand the voluntary vs. compelled action
The author distinguishes between a voluntary agent (who can do the opposite) and a compelled agent (who cannot). Denying God the ability to change fire's properties implies He is compelled, which is impermissible.
This distinction is central to the argument.
4
Reflect on the miraculous examples
The author lists miracles like bringing a camel from a rock, water from fingers, etc. These show that God's power is not limited by created natures.
If God can create the world from nothing, He can certainly change properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم : أنكم ساعدتمونا على أن جميع ما وجد في العالم مخلوق الله تعالى عرضا كان ذلك أو جوهرا، وأنه تعالى موصوف بالقدرة على ما أراد إيجاده إن النار جسم خلقه الله تعالى وخلق فيه الحرارة واليبوسة، فمن زعم أنه لا يقدر على أن يخلق هو بدل الحرارة فيها البرودة وبدل اليبوسة الرطوبة، فقد وصف الله تعالى بالعجز، وجعل أفعاله ضرورية لا اختيارية، إذ المختار والفعل كان سبيل من فعل ضد ما فعله إذ هو بذلك يمتاز عن المضطر، ووصف الله تعالى بالعجز والاضطرار جهل فاحش وكذا الله تعالى لما كان قادرا على اختراع العالم لا عن أصل، لماذا لا يوصف بالقدرة على إخراج الناقة من الحجر، وإخراج الماء من بين الأصابع، وإيجاد الحياة في العصا، وتحويل أجزاء الخشب لحما وغير ذلك.
Then we say to them: You have agreed with us that all that exists in the world is created by God Almighty, whether it be an accident or a substance, and that He is described with power over what He wills to bring into existence. Fire is a body created by God Almighty, and He created in it heat and dryness. So whoever claims that He is not able to create instead of heat in it cold, and instead of dryness wetness, has described God Almighty with incapacity, and has made His actions necessary not voluntary. For the one who chooses and acts is one who can do the opposite of what he did, because by that he is distinguished from the one who is compelled. Describing God Almighty with incapacity and compulsion is gross ignorance. Likewise, since God Almighty is powerful to invent the world without an origin, why should He not be described with power to bring forth a she-camel from a rock, to bring forth water from between fingers, to create life in a staff, and to transform parts of wood into flesh, and other such things?
Source pages p. 704

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the naturalists by focusing on God's power. The author first points out that the naturalists themselves admit that everything in the world—both accidents and substances—is created by God. Therefore, they must also admit that God has power over His creation. If someone claims that God cannot change the properties of fire (e.g., making it cold or wet), then that person is attributing impotence (العجز) to God and implying that God's actions are necessary and involuntary. A voluntary agent can do the opposite of what he does; a compelled agent cannot. Describing God with incapacity and compulsion is gross ignorance. Since God created the entire world from nothing, He surely has the power to perform miracles like bringing a camel from a rock, water from between fingers, life to a staff, or turning wood into flesh. Thus, denying such miracles implies a deficiency in God's power.

Key Terms
العجز
الاضطرار
المختار
المضطر
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot change natural properties because nature is fixed and follows its own laws.
Correction: The passage argues that such a belief attributes impotence and compulsion to God. Since God created nature from nothing, He is able to alter it at will. Denying this implies a limitation on God's absolute power.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does someone who claims God cannot change the properties of fire (e.g., make it cold) actually do?
A) Recognize the fixed nature of fire.
B) Describe God with incapacity and compulsion.
C) Affirm God's voluntary action.
D) Deny God's existence.

Key Takeaway
Denying God's power to change nature is equivalent to describing Him with weakness and compulsion, which is gross ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you find yourself thinking 'this situation will never change because it's just the way it is'? How does this lesson challenge that assumption about God's power?
?
If you truly believed that God can change any natural outcome without needing natural means, how would your prayers for difficult situations change starting today?
?
When you face a dead-end circumstance, do you first consider that God might perform a miracle, or do you default to human limitation? What does that reveal about your view of God's voluntary power?

The Proof from Substance and Motion
Proof using analogy of substance and motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that subsistence (al-baqā') is an additional attribute over the essence, using the analogy of motion and the consensus of scholars.

Core Idea
Subsistence is additional because it is absent at first existence, like motion.

The author begins by narrowing the discussion to the key debate with the Mu'tazila: is subsistence an additional attribute or identical to the essence? He then presents a clear proof based on the observation that a substance in its first moment of existence is not described as 'subsistent', and uses the analogy of motion to demonstrate that subsistence must be an additional meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main debate
Read the first part where the author specifies the debate with the Mu'tazila about subsistence. Note the consensus that accidents are described by essential attributes.
2
Understand the proof's starting point
The proof begins with the claim that a substance in its first moment of existence is not described as 'subsistent'. This is agreed upon by both parties, as shown by the cited Mu'tazila definitions.
Even al-Jubbā'ī and Abū Hāshim's definitions imply subsistence is not present at first.
3
Follow the analogy with motion
Just as motion is an additional attribute because the substance is first without motion then acquires it, subsistence follows the same pattern.
This is the core analogy of the proof.
4
Reflect on the consequence
If subsistence were not additional, the description would never separate from the essence, but we see it does—so it must be additional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلام مع النظام يقع في كون الألوان والطعوم والروائح والأصوات وغير ذلك أعراضاً أو أجسامًا، وذلك خارج عن غرضنا، وقد ذكرناه قبل هذا. وكذا الكلام مع الضرارية والنجارية يقع في بيان إحالة كون الأعراض أبعاضًا للأجسام، والكلام مع بقية المعتزلة يقع في أن البقاء هل هو معنى زائد على ذات الباقي أم لا؟ عندنا هو معنى زائد على ذات الباقي، وعندهم هو راجع إلى ذاته. هذا هو محل النزاع لتقدم الإجماع: أن الأعراض توصف بما كان راجعا إلى الذات كالوجود والشيئية واللونية والعرضية، ولا توصف بما كان معنى زائدًا على الذات والدليل على أن البقاء معنى زائد على ذات الباقي أن الجوهر في أول أحوال وجوده غير مستحق للوصف بالبقاء انعقد عليه الإجماع بيننا وبين الجمهور من حذاقهم حتى قال أبو علي الجبائي: البقاء هو الوجود عن وجوده. وقال أصحاب أبي هاشم الباقي هو الموجود الذي قد أتى عليه زمان، وحكى مثل هذا القول عن أبي الحسين الخياط رئيس البغدادية من المعتزلة، وقال بعض أصحاب أبي هاشم الباقي هو الموجود الذي لم يحدث في حال الإخبار بالوجود، ثم يوصف بعد ذلك بالبقاء، ولو لم يكن البقاء معنى زائدًا على الذات لما انفك الوصف بالبقاء عن وجود الذات كما في الوجود، وعكسه الحركة فإن الجوهر في أول أحوال وجوده لما كان غير موصوف بالحركة ثم اتصف بعد ذلك بها علم أنها معنى زائد على الذات فكذا البقاء، ولو بطل أن يكون البقاء معنى زائدًا على مع وجود الذات تارة متعريًا عن الوصف به بل مستحيل الوصف به لبطلت الدلالة على ثبوت الأعراض بطلان الدلالة على حدوث العالم إذ بثبوتها وتحقق حدوثها يستدل على حدوث الجواهر. والذي يحقق هذا أن عبارة القدرية في إثبات التفرقة بين صفة النفس وبين صفة المعنى أن صفة النفس ما لزمه ووصف المعنى ما جاز خروجه
And the discussion with al-Naẓẓām concerns whether colors, tastes, smells, sounds, and other things are accidents or bodies, and that is outside our purpose, and we have mentioned it before this. Likewise, the discussion with al-Ḍirāriyya and al-Najjāriyya concerns the demonstration of the impossibility of accidents being parts of bodies. And the discussion with the rest of the Muʿtazila concerns whether subsistence (al-baqāʾ) is an additional meaning (maʿnan zāʾid) over the essence of the subsistent or not. With us, it is an additional meaning over the essence of the subsistent; with them, it refers back to its essence. This is the point of contention, due to the prior consensus that accidents are described by what refers back to the essence, like existence (al-wujūd), thingness (al-shayʾiyya), color-ness (al-lawniyya), and accident-ness (al-ʿaraḍiyya), and are not described by what is an additional meaning over the essence. And the proof that subsistence is an additional meaning over the essence of the subsistent is that the substance (al-jawhar) in the first state of its existence is not deserving of the description of subsistence—on this there is consensus between us and the majority of their experts—so much so that Abū ʿAlī al-Jubbāʾī said: subsistence is existence from its existence. And the followers of Abū Hāshim said: the subsistent is the existent upon which time has passed. And a similar statement is related from Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Khayyāṭ, the leader of the Baghdādiyya of the Muʿtazila. And some followers of Abū Hāshim said: the subsistent is the existent that has not come to be at the time of reporting existence, then it is afterwards described by subsistence. And if subsistence were not an additional meaning over the essence, the description of subsistence would not cease to accompany the existence of the essence, as in the case of existence. And the opposite is motion; for the substance in the first state of its existence, since it is not described by motion and is afterwards described by it, it is known that it (motion) is an additional meaning over the essence. So too is subsistence. And if it were invalid that subsistence is an additional meaning, sometimes the existence of the essence would be stripped of the description of it, rather the description would be impossible for it, which would invalidate the proof for the establishment of accidents, and the invalidation of the proof for the temporal origination of the world; for by their establishment and the verification of their temporal origination, the temporal origination of substances is inferred. And what confirms this is the statement of the Qadariyya in establishing the distinction between the attribute of the self (ṣifat al-nafs) and the attribute of meaning (ṣifat al-maʿnā): that the attribute of the self is what is necessary for it, and the attribute of meaning is what can be removed from it.
Source pages p. 796

Commentary
This passage is a combined presentation of discussions with various sects, culminating in a proof that subsistence (al-baqāʾ) is an additional attribute (maʿnan zāʾid) over the essence. The author begins by noting that the discussion with al-Naẓẓām about colors etc. being accidents or bodies is outside the present purpose, and likewise the discussion with al-Ḍirāriyya and al-Najjāriyya about accidents being parts of bodies is also separate. The main debate is with the Muʿtazila: whether subsistence is an additional meaning over the essence of the subsistent or merely refers back to it. The author states that the Ashʿarī/Māturīdī position is that it is additional, while the Muʿtazila say it refers back. The point of contention is precedent by consensus that accidents are described by what refers back to the essence (like existence, thingness, color-ness, accident-ness) and not by what is additional. Then the author provides the proof: the substance (jawhar) in its first moment of existence is not qualified by subsistence—this is agreed upon by both parties. Various Muʿtazilī definitions of 'subsistent' are cited: al-Jubbāʾī says subsistence is 'existence from its existence'; the followers of Abū Hāshim say it is 'the existent upon which time has passed'; al-Khayyāṭ says similar; some say it is 'the existent that has not come to be at the time of reporting existence, then afterwards described by subsistence'. If subsistence were not an additional meaning, the description of subsistence would never separate from the existence of the essence, just as existence itself never separates. But we observe that a substance at first exists without being described as 'subsistent', then later it is so described. This is analogous to motion: a substance in its first state is not described by motion, then later it is described by it, proving motion is an additional meaning. Similarly, subsistence must be an additional meaning. If this were denied, it would imply that sometimes the essence exists stripped of the description of subsistence, which would be impossible if subsistence were not additional, and this would undermine the proof for accidents and thus the proof for the temporal origination of the world (since accidents' temporality proves substances' temporality). Finally, the author notes that this distinction is confirmed by the Qadariyya's (i.e., Muʿtazila's) own distinction between attribute of the self (ṣifat al-nafs) and attribute of meaning (ṣifat al-maʿnā): the former is necessary to the essence, the latter can be absent. Since subsistence can be absent at first existence, it falls under attribute of meaning, hence additional.

Key Terms
بقاء
ذات
معنى زائد
جوهر
حركة
إجماع
أبو علي الجبائي
أبو هاشم
أبو الحسين الخياط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Subsistence is inherent to existence itself.
Correction: The proof shows it is an additional attribute because it is absent at the first moment of existence, just like motion.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to prove that subsistence is an additional attribute?
A) Analogy of existence and thingness
B) Analogy of motion and substance
C) Analogy of color and taste
D) Analogy of will and power

Key Takeaway
Subsistence is an additional attribute, proven by the fact that a substance at first exists without being described as 'subsistent', analogously to motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very persistence from one moment to the next is not intrinsic to you but a gift added to your existence, how often do you consciously thank Allah for keeping you in existence this very second? What would change in your prayers today if you truly felt that your next breath is not owed to you?
?
The proof shows that at the first moment of existence, a substance is not yet 'subsistent'. Reflect: Are you living as if your continued existence is guaranteed, or do you wake each morning aware that you are being re-created anew? How can you make this awareness a practical reality before you sleep tonight?
?
This argument treats subsistence as an additional attribute, just like motion. Consider: if even your ability to remain alive is not part of your essence, what claim do you have to any of your qualities as 'yours'? Will you let this truth strip away your pride today and turn you back to the One who gives you persistence?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Mu'tazila Definitions and the Attribute Distinction
Cites Mu'tazila definitions and the distinction between attribute of self and attribute of meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila definitions of subsistence and their own distinction between attribute of self and attribute of meaning actually support the Ash'arī/Māturīdī position that subsistence is an additional attribute.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila admit subsistence is not present at first, confirming it is an attribute of meaning.

After presenting the proof, the author draws out its consequences: if subsistence were not additional, the entire proof for the temporality of the world would collapse. He then appeals to the Qadariyya's (Mu'tazila) own distinction between attribute of self (necessary) and attribute of meaning (accidental) to confirm that subsistence falls into the latter category, hence it is an additional attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the consequence of denial
The author argues that if subsistence were not additional, it would invalidate the proof for accidents and, consequently, the proof for the world's temporal origination.
This shows the high stakes of the debate.
2
Learn the Qadariyya distinction
The Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) distinguish between attribute of self (ṣifat al-nafs) which is necessary and inseparable, and attribute of meaning (ṣifat al-maʿnā) which can be removed.
This is a distinction they themselves use.
3
Apply the distinction to subsistence
Since subsistence can be absent at the first moment of existence, it is an attribute of meaning, not of self. Therefore, it is an additional attribute over the essence.
This reinforces the proof from the previous lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلام مع النظام يقع في كون الألوان والطعوم والروائح والأصوات وغير ذلك أعراضاً أو أجسامًا، وذلك خارج عن غرضنا، وقد ذكرناه قبل هذا. وكذا الكلام مع الضرارية والنجارية يقع في بيان إحالة كون الأعراض أبعاضًا للأجسام، والكلام مع بقية المعتزلة يقع في أن البقاء هل هو معنى زائد على ذات الباقي أم لا؟ عندنا هو معنى زائد على ذات الباقي، وعندهم هو راجع إلى ذاته. هذا هو محل النزاع لتقدم الإجماع: أن الأعراض توصف بما كان راجعا إلى الذات كالوجود والشيئية واللونية والعرضية، ولا توصف بما كان معنى زائدًا على الذات والدليل على أن البقاء معنى زائد على ذات الباقي أن الجوهر في أول أحوال وجوده غير مستحق للوصف بالبقاء انعقد عليه الإجماع بيننا وبين الجمهور من حذاقهم حتى قال أبو علي الجبائي: البقاء هو الوجود عن وجوده. وقال أصحاب أبي هاشم الباقي هو الموجود الذي قد أتى عليه زمان، وحكى مثل هذا القول عن أبي الحسين الخياط رئيس البغدادية من المعتزلة، وقال بعض أصحاب أبي هاشم الباقي هو الموجود الذي لم يحدث في حال الإخبار بالوجود، ثم يوصف بعد ذلك بالبقاء، ولو لم يكن البقاء معنى زائدًا على الذات لما انفك الوصف بالبقاء عن وجود الذات كما في الوجود، وعكسه الحركة فإن الجوهر في أول أحوال وجوده لما كان غير موصوف بالحركة ثم اتصف بعد ذلك بها علم أنها معنى زائد على الذات فكذا البقاء، ولو بطل أن يكون البقاء معنى زائدًا على مع وجود الذات تارة متعريًا عن الوصف به بل مستحيل الوصف به لبطلت الدلالة على ثبوت الأعراض بطلان الدلالة على حدوث العالم إذ بثبوتها وتحقق حدوثها يستدل على حدوث الجواهر. والذي يحقق هذا أن عبارة القدرية في إثبات التفرقة بين صفة النفس وبين صفة المعنى أن صفة النفس ما لزمه ووصف المعنى ما جاز خروجه
And the discussion with al-Naẓẓām concerns whether colors, tastes, smells, sounds, and other things are accidents or bodies, and that is outside our purpose, and we have mentioned it before this. Likewise, the discussion with al-Ḍirāriyya and al-Najjāriyya concerns the demonstration of the impossibility of accidents being parts of bodies. And the discussion with the rest of the Muʿtazila concerns whether subsistence (al-baqāʾ) is an additional meaning (maʿnan zāʾid) over the essence of the subsistent or not. With us, it is an additional meaning over the essence of the subsistent; with them, it refers back to its essence. This is the point of contention, due to the prior consensus that accidents are described by what refers back to the essence, like existence (al-wujūd), thingness (al-shayʾiyya), color-ness (al-lawniyya), and accident-ness (al-ʿaraḍiyya), and are not described by what is an additional meaning over the essence. And the proof that subsistence is an additional meaning over the essence of the subsistent is that the substance (al-jawhar) in the first state of its existence is not deserving of the description of subsistence—on this there is consensus between us and the majority of their experts—so much so that Abū ʿAlī al-Jubbāʾī said: subsistence is existence from its existence. And the followers of Abū Hāshim said: the subsistent is the existent upon which time has passed. And a similar statement is related from Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Khayyāṭ, the leader of the Baghdādiyya of the Muʿtazila. And some followers of Abū Hāshim said: the subsistent is the existent that has not come to be at the time of reporting existence, then it is afterwards described by subsistence. And if subsistence were not an additional meaning over the essence, the description of subsistence would not cease to accompany the existence of the essence, as in the case of existence. And the opposite is motion; for the substance in the first state of its existence, since it is not described by motion and is afterwards described by it, it is known that it (motion) is an additional meaning over the essence. So too is subsistence. And if it were invalid that subsistence is an additional meaning, sometimes the existence of the essence would be stripped of the description of it, rather the description would be impossible for it, which would invalidate the proof for the establishment of accidents, and the invalidation of the proof for the temporal origination of the world; for by their establishment and the verification of their temporal origination, the temporal origination of substances is inferred. And what confirms this is the statement of the Qadariyya in establishing the distinction between the attribute of the self (ṣifat al-nafs) and the attribute of meaning (ṣifat al-maʿnā): that the attribute of the self is what is necessary for it, and the attribute of meaning is what can be removed from it.
Source pages p. 796

Commentary
This passage is a combined presentation of discussions with various sects, culminating in a proof that subsistence (al-baqāʾ) is an additional attribute (maʿnan zāʾid) over the essence. The author begins by noting that the discussion with al-Naẓẓām about colors etc. being accidents or bodies is outside the present purpose, and likewise the discussion with al-Ḍirāriyya and al-Najjāriyya about accidents being parts of bodies is also separate. The main debate is with the Muʿtazila: whether subsistence is an additional meaning over the essence of the subsistent or merely refers back to it. The author states that the Ashʿarī/Māturīdī position is that it is additional, while the Muʿtazila say it refers back. The point of contention is precedent by consensus that accidents are described by what refers back to the essence (like existence, thingness, color-ness, accident-ness) and not by what is additional. Then the author provides the proof: the substance (jawhar) in its first moment of existence is not qualified by subsistence—this is agreed upon by both parties. Various Muʿtazilī definitions of 'subsistent' are cited: al-Jubbāʾī says subsistence is 'existence from its existence'; the followers of Abū Hāshim say it is 'the existent upon which time has passed'; al-Khayyāṭ says similar; some say it is 'the existent that has not come to be at the time of reporting existence, then afterwards described by subsistence'. If subsistence were not an additional meaning, the description of subsistence would never separate from the existence of the essence, just as existence itself never separates. But we observe that a substance at first exists without being described as 'subsistent', then later it is so described. This is analogous to motion: a substance in its first state is not described by motion, then later it is described by it, proving motion is an additional meaning. Similarly, subsistence must be an additional meaning. If this were denied, it would imply that sometimes the essence exists stripped of the description of subsistence, which would be impossible if subsistence were not additional, and this would undermine the proof for accidents and thus the proof for the temporal origination of the world (since accidents' temporality proves substances' temporality). Finally, the author notes that this distinction is confirmed by the Qadariyya's (i.e., Muʿtazila's) own distinction between attribute of the self (ṣifat al-nafs) and attribute of meaning (ṣifat al-maʿnā): the former is necessary to the essence, the latter can be absent. Since subsistence can be absent at first existence, it falls under attribute of meaning, hence additional.

Key Terms
بقاء
ذات
معنى زائد
جوهر
صفة النفس
صفة المعنى
قدرية
أعراض
حدوث العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila unanimously deny that subsistence is an additional attribute.
Correction: Their own definitions and distinction between attribute of self and attribute of meaning actually confirm that subsistence is additional, since it can be absent at first.

Checkpoint
According to the Qadariyya, how is the attribute of meaning (ṣifat al-maʿnā) defined?
A) What is necessary for the essence
B) What can be removed from the essence
C) What is inherent to the essence
D) What is eternal

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's own distinction between attribute of self and attribute of meaning shows that subsistence, being removable at first existence, is an additional attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a belief about yourself—like 'I'm just not good enough'—as if it were an essential part of you, when actually it is removable like an attribute of meaning? What would it mean to treat it as removable today?
?
The Mu'tazila's own distinction forced them to accept a truth they otherwise rejected. Is there a truth about your relationship with Allah that your own reasoning avoids, even though your standards demand it? Will you accept it now?
?
The source shows that denying one additional attribute unravels the entire proof for the world's creation. In your life, are there small compromises you make that, if followed consistently, would collapse your entire faith? Which one will you address today?

Proof that Permanence is a Superadded Attribute
Nasafi adduces proof that permanence (baqa') is an additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the initial state of atoms and the analogy of movement to prove that permanence is a superadded attribute distinct from existence.

Core Idea
Permanence cannot be the same as existence because atoms begin without it.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents a key proof for the reality of divine attributes. He argues from the observable world — specifically the behavior of atoms and accidents — to show that permanence (baqa') is an added meaning, not identical to existence. The argument is crucial because denying this would undermine the entire cosmological proof for the temporal origination of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Focus on understanding the overall flow: Nasafi starts with a consensus, then cites Mu'tazilite definitions, then uses an analogy with movement, and finally warns of the consequences.
Notice that the passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the consensus and definitions
Find the statement that the atom at its first moment of existence is not described as permanent. Then note the different Mu'tazilite definitions of 'permanently existing' — they all imply that permanence is acquired after existence.
The author quotes these definitions not to endorse them, but to show they admit permanence as separate.
3
Trace the analogy with movement
See how the author compares permanence to movement: an atom initially lacks movement but later acquires it, proving movement is a superadded meaning. The same logic applies to permanence.
This is the core analogical argument.
4
Understand the consequence
Read the final sentence: if permanence is not superadded, then the proof for accidents collapses, and hence the proof for the temporal origination of the world. This shows the high stakes of this attribute.
This connects the attribute to the broader theological system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أن البقاء معنى زائد على ذات الباقي أن الجوهر في أول أحوال وجوده غير مستحق للوصف بالبقاء انعقد عليه الإجماع بيننا وبين الجمهور من حذاقهم حتى قال أبو علي الجبائي: البقاء هو الوجود عن وجوده. وقال أصحاب أبي هاشم الباقي هو الموجود الذي قد أتى عليه زمان، وحكى مثل هذا القول عن أبي الحسين الخياط رئيس البغدادية من المعتزلة، وقال بعض أصحاب أبي هاشم الباقي هو الموجود الذي لم يحدث في حال الإخبار بالوجود، ثم يوصف بعد ذلك بالبقاء، ولو لم يكن البقاء معنى زائدًا على الذات لما انفك الوصف بالبقاء عن وجود الذات كما في الوجود، وعكسه الحركة فإن الجوهر في أول أحوال وجوده لما كان غير موصوف بالحركة ثم اتصف بعد ذلك بها علم أنها معنى زائد على الذات فكذا البقاء، ولو بطل أن يكون البقاء معنى زائدًا على مع وجود الذات تارة متعريًا عن الوصف به بل مستحيل الوصف به لبطلت الدلالة على ثبوت الأعراض بطلان الدلالة على حدوث العالم إذ بثبوتها وتحقق حدوثها يستدل على حدوث الجواهر.
And the proof that permanence is a superadded meaning upon the essence of the Permanently Existing is that the atom (jawhar) at the first state of its existence is not deserving of the description of permanence; consensus has been established upon this between us and the majority of their experts, so that Abu Ali al-Jubba'i said: 'Permanence is existence from its existence.' And the followers of Abu Hashim said: 'The Permanently Existing is the existing thing upon which time has passed.' And a similar statement was narrated from Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat, the head of the Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila. And some followers of Abu Hashim said: 'The Permanently Existing is the existing thing that has not come into existence at the time of reporting its existence, then it is described after that with permanence.' And if permanence were not a superadded meaning upon the essence, the description of permanence would never be separate from the existence of the essence, as is the case with existence. And the opposite is movement: for the atom, at the first state of its existence, since it was not described with movement, then was described with it afterwards, it was known that it is a superadded meaning upon the essence. So too is permanence. And if it were invalid that permanence be a superadded meaning upon [with?] the existence of the essence, at times being divested of the description of it, rather impossible of being described with it, then the indication of the subsistence of accidents would be invalidated, and the invalidation of the indication of the temporal origination of the world—since by their subsistence and the verification of their temporal origination one infers the temporal origination of atoms.
Source pages p. 797

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that permanence (بقاء) is a meaning added to the essence. The argument begins: an atom (الجوهر) in its first moment of existence is not deserving of the description 'permanent'—this is agreed upon by the author's school and the majority of the Mu'tazila. The author cites various Mu'tazilite definitions of the 'permanently existing' to show they all imply that permanence is acquired after existence, not co-extensive with it. Next, he argues: if permanence were not an added meaning, the description of permanence would never be separated from the essence—like existence itself, which is inseparable from an existing thing. But we observe the opposite with movement: an atom that is not moving at first becomes moving later; this shows movement is an added attribute. Similarly, because the atom initially lacks permanence and then acquires it, permanence must also be an added meaning. Finally, he warns: if one denies that permanence is a superadded meaning, then the proof for the reality of accidents collapses, and consequently the proof for the temporal origination of the world (which relies on accidents) fails. Thus the whole cosmological argument is at stake.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqa': permanence)
معنى زائد (ma'na za'id: superadded meaning)
جوهر (jawhar: atom/substance)
عرض ('arad: accident)
حدوث العالم (huduth al-'alam: temporal origination of the world)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite definitions quoted show that they deny permanence as a separate attribute.
Correction: The author quotes these definitions to demonstrate that even the Mu'tazila implicitly admit permanence is something acquired after existence, thus supporting his argument that it is a superadded meaning.

Checkpoint
Why does the author use the analogy of movement to prove that permanence is a superadded meaning?
A) Because movement and permanence are both essential attributes of the atom.
B) Because the atom initially lacks movement, then acquires it, proving movement is a superadded meaning—likewise permanence.
C) Because movement is the opposite of permanence, so the analogy shows contrast.
D) Because both movement and permanence are eternal attributes of God.

Key Takeaway
Permanence is a superadded attribute because atoms initially lack it but later possess it, just like movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah alone is truly permanent, and everything else begins its existence without permanence, then what in your life are you treating as if it will last forever? Name one specific attachment you hold onto that, in light of this lesson, you must now release to Allah.
?
The atom at its first moment lacks permanence—it depends entirely on the Permanent One. When you woke up this morning, did you live as though your next breath depends on the One who gives permanence? What would change in your morning if you truly realized you begin each moment without any guarantee of continuation?
?
The proof that permanence is a superadded attribute reveals that existence itself does not guarantee continuity. If your existence is a gift that could be withdrawn at any instant by the Permanent, what act of obedience have you been delaying that you will now commit to starting today, not assuming you will have tomorrow?

Objection and Refutation on Necessary Change and Added Meanings
Nasafi refutes the objection that necessary change does not require a superadded meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the objection that a necessary change (where the atom never exists without the attribute) does not prove a superadded meaning, using the principle that attribution is either from the essence or from a meaning.

Core Idea
Even necessary change requires a distinct cause when the essence alone cannot produce the attribute.

This passage presents an important objection in kalam: if an attribute like permanence is always present from the moment of creation, does its emergence still require an additional meaning? The objector argues no, but Nasafi responds with a logical principle that establishes the need for a distinct cause. By working through this objection and its refutation, you will see how the Maturidi tradition defends the doctrine of superadded attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the objector's claim: if the atom never exists without permanence, then the change does not prove a new meaning. Note the condition 'if it is possible for the atom to exist divested of that description at times'.
The objector assumes that a necessary and immediate change does not need a cause beyond the essence.
2
Study the refutation's key principle
Nasafi states that attribution is either from the essence or from a meaning. When the essence exists and the attribution is impossible, it cannot be from the essence. Therefore, it must be from a meaning. Reflect on why this holds even if the impossibility is only for a moment.
The phrase 'موجبات الذات' (necessitants of the essence) is central.
3
Analyze the logical argument
Notice the conditional reasoning: if the attribution were from the essence, it would be present whenever the essence exists. Since it is not (even briefly), it cannot be from the essence. Then consider the analogy of blackness: it is absurd to say blackness subsists but cannot be described as black initially, then later it can.
The analogy shows the objection leads to a contradiction.
4
Connect to the broader lesson
Understand why this argument is important: it defends the view that even essential attributes (like existence's permanence) are superadded meanings, not identical with the essence. This has implications for divine attributes in kalam.
This refutation strengthens the case for attributes being distinct from the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسأل بعضهم: إن تغير الجوهر عما كان عليه إنما يوجب التغير بوجود معنى زائد على الذات إذا جاز وجود الجوهر متعريًا عن ذلك الوصف أوقاتًا، فأما إذا لم يجز تعريه عنه ولا وقتا واحدًا ووجب تغيره في الثاني من حال حدوثه لا محالة لم يجب به حدوث معنى فيه. قلنا هذا كلام من لا يدري ما يقول، وهذا لأن الاتصاف إما أن يكون لأجل الذات، وإما أن يكون لمعنى، وإذا وجد الذات واستحال الاتصاف علم أن الاتصاف ليس من موجبات الذات، فعلم أنه من موجبات المعنى لأن الذات لو وجد وانعدم جواز الاتصاف كان الاتصاف من موجبات المعنى دون الذات، فإذا كان الاتصاف مستحيلاً مع وجود الذات كان أولى أن يكون الاتصاف موجب معنى وراء الذات لا موجب الذات. ويحققه أن الاتصاف لو كان من موجبات الذات وقد وجدت الذات واستحال الاتصاف لكان فيه القول باستحالة ثبوت الحكم، وإن تحققت العلة وهذا ظاهر الفساد ولو جاز هذا لجاز أن يقوم السواد بذات ويستحيل اتصافه بأنه أسود ثم في الزمان الثاني يجب اتصافه به وهذا محال لم يقل به أحد من العقلاء.
And someone asked: 'The change of the atom from what it was only necessitates change by the existence of a superadded meaning upon the essence if it is possible for the atom to exist divested of that description at times. But if it is not possible for it to be divested of it for even one time, and its change in the second [moment] from the state of its temporal origination is necessary without fail, then it does not necessitate the occurrence of a meaning in it.' We said: This is the speech of one who does not know what he is saying. This is because the attribution is either because of the essence or because of a meaning. And when the essence exists and the attribution is impossible, it is known that the attribution is not from the necessary requirements of the essence; therefore it is known that it is from the necessary requirements of a meaning. For if the essence existed and the possibility of the attribution were absent, the attribution would be from the necessary requirements of the meaning, not the essence. And if the attribution is impossible while the essence exists, it is even more proper that the attribution be the necessitation of a meaning beyond the essence, not the necessitation of the essence. And what verifies it is that if the attribution were from the necessary requirements of the essence and the essence existed while the attribution is impossible, then this would involve saying that it is impossible for the judgment to subsist even though the cause is realized—and this is obviously corrupt. And if this were allowed, it would be allowed for blackness to subsist in a substance while it is impossible for it to be described as black, then in the second moment its being described with it becomes necessary—and this is impossible, and no rational person has ever said it.
Source pages p. 798

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding whether a necessary change (not merely possible) still proves an added attribute. The objector argues: if the atom necessarily changes from not having permanence to having it, and it never could exist without permanence (even for an instant), then this change does not prove a superadded meaning because there is no time when the atom is divested of permanence to contrast. The author refutes this strongly, calling it ignorance. He explains a logical principle: attribution (الاتصاف) is either due to the essence itself or due to an additional meaning. If the essence exists and the attribution is impossible, then the attribution cannot be from the essence (since if it were, it would be present whenever the essence is). Therefore it must be from a meaning. The impossibility of the attribution at the first moment, even if brief, is enough to prove the attribution is not essential. He provides a clarifying analogy: if blackness were in a substance but it were impossible for it to be described as black initially, then impossible for the description to appear later, this would be absurd. No rational person accepts that a cause (the essence) exists but the effect (the attribute) cannot be present, only to appear later without a new cause. Thus the objection fails, and permanence must be a superadded meaning.

Key Terms
تغير (taghayyur: change)
الاتصاف (al-ittisaf: attribution)
موجبات الذات (mujibat al-dhat: necessitants of the essence)
موجبات المعنى (mujibat al-ma'na: necessitants of the meaning)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a change is necessary and immediate, it does not need a cause distinct from the essence.
Correction: Even if the attribute appears at the first moment, the impossibility of its absence (even for an instant) while the essence exists proves the attribute is not essential, so a distinct cause (a meaning) is required.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does the impossibility of an attribution while the essence exists prove?
A) The attribution is from the essence.
B) The attribution is from a superadded meaning.
C) The essence cannot exist without the attribution.
D) The objection is valid.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of an attribute at any moment while the essence exists proves that the attribute is from a distinct meaning, not from the essence alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a fault in your character that you have always assumed is 'just who you are' and therefore unchangeable? This lesson teaches that even constant attributes have a cause. What is that cause for you, and what step can you take today to address it?
?
Think of a blessing you experience daily—like your health or faith. Do you treat it as automatic, or do you acknowledge the cause behind it? What meaningful action can you take to preserve or increase it?
?
The objector argued that if something is always there, it needs no cause. In your spiritual life, have you neglected the means that sustain your good habits? Identify one good habit you have that seems constant but might weaken if you stop maintaining its cause.

Divine Attributes
advanced
6 min
The Essence Cannot Necessitate Attributes Without a Change
Nasafi uses reductio and dilemma against attribute denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the essence alone cannot necessitate attributes, and how the dilemma about substance shows the need for a distinct meaning.

Core Idea
Attributes require a meaning; the essence alone cannot necessitate them without change.

Imam Nasafi begins by exposing the absurd consequences of claiming the essence can necessitate an attribute in a second state without a new meaning. He then poses a crucial dilemma to his opponents: why does a substance become qualified by a description? This lesson unpacks these foundational arguments that establish the necessity of a meaning (ma‘nā) for the acquisition of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage divides into two distinct arguments.
2
Identify the reductio argument
The first argument is a reductio ad absurdum. Find the impossible consequence the author derives from the opponent's position.
The consequence involves incapacity and ignorance.
3
Understand the dilemma
The second argument presents a dilemma about why a substance becomes qualified by a description. Consider both options and their implications.
The opponent's choice reveals a flaw in his objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أن الذات لو لم توجب وجود الاتصاف في الأول ثم في الثاني عين ذلك الذات الذي لم يوجب الاتصاف واستحال وجود الاتصاف معه أوجب الاتصاف من غير تغيير ولا حدوث معنى فيه لوجب أن يقال : إن العجز وإن وجد في أول أحوال وجوده و استحال اتصاف الذات الذي قام به العجز بكونه قادرًا يوجب في الثاني كونه قادرا ، وكذا الذات الذي قام به الجهل يوجب كون الذات في الثاني عالمًا وإن استحال اتصافه في أول الأحوال، وهذا محال.
ثم يقال لهم : الوصف الذي كان يجوز كون الجوهر متعريًا عند أوقاتا لم كان وصف المعنى؟ ألأجل أنه إذا انعدم مع وجود الذات علم أنه ليس من موجبات الذات أم لأجل معنى آخر؟
فإن قال بالثاني، كلف إبرازه ولا قدره له عليه، وإن قال بالأول فقد أقر بما يوجب بطلان سؤاله هذا، وظهر أن لا فرق بين جواز التعري وقتا واحدًا وبين جوازه أوقاتا كثيرة.
ثم يقال : لو كان بغير الوصف مع جواز بقائه متعريا عنه يوجب حدوث معنى كان بغيره مع استحالة بقائه متعريًا عنه أولى، ولولا حدوث المعنى الذي اضطر إلى تغير وصفه لجاز وجوده على ما كان عليه قبله.
ثم يقال لهم : إذا استحال كون الجوهر متحركًا أو ساكنا في حال الحدوث و استحال بقاؤه عليه متعريًا عنهما بعد لم يحتج إلى حدوث معنى من أجله صار متحركًا أو ساكنا، فإذا كان هذا مستحيلاً ولم يكن جائزا عند أهل التوحيد ولم يكن بد من القول بوجود الحركة أو السكون دل ذلك على بعض ما قلتموه.
He verifies that if the essence did not necessitate the existence of qualification in the first state, and then in the second state the same essence which did not necessitate qualification and the existence of qualification with it is impossible, necessitated qualification without change or occurrence of a meaning in it, then it would have to be said: Incapacity, even if it exists in the first of its states of existence and the qualification of the essence in which incapacity inheres with being powerful is impossible, necessitates in the second state that it be powerful; and likewise the essence in which ignorance inheres necessitates that the essence be knowing in the second state, even though its qualification is impossible in the first state, and this is impossible. Then it is said to them: The description (al-waṣf) which the substance (al-jawhar) could be devoid of at times? Why was the description of the meaning? Is it because when it is absent with the existence of the essence, it is known that it is not from the necessitations of the essence, or for another meaning? If he says the second, he is charged to produce it and he has no power over it; and if he says the first, then he has admitted what necessitates the invalidity of this question of his, and it appears that there is no difference between the permissibility of being devoid at one time and the permissibility of being devoid at many times. Then it is said: If that which is without a description, with the permissibility of its remaining devoid of it, necessitates the occurrence of a meaning, then that which is without a description with the impossibility of its remaining devoid of it is more deserving; and were it not for the occurrence of the meaning which compelled a change in its description, its existence would be permissible as it was before. Then it is said to them: If it is impossible for the substance to be moving or resting at the moment of origination, and impossible for it to remain upon it devoid of them afterwards, it would not need the occurrence of a meaning for which it became moving or resting. If this is impossible and not permissible according to the people of tawhid, and there is no escape from affirming the existence of motion or rest, that indicates some of what you have said.
Source pages p. 799

Commentary
This passage is a series of proofs by reductio ad absurdum against those who claim that attributes (such as power and knowledge) are necessitated by the essence itself without a distinct meaning (ma‘nā). The author begins by stating that if the essence did not necessitate the attribute (i.e., qualification) in the first state, then the same essence in the second state—without any change or new meaning—cannot necessitate it. If it did, then incapacity (ʿajz) would necessitate power, and ignorance (jahl) would necessitate knowledge, which is absurd. Next, the author poses a dilemma to the opponent: why does a substance (jawhar) that could be devoid of a description at some times become qualified by a meaning? Either because the absence of the description with the existence of the essence shows it is not necessitated by the essence, or for another reason. If the opponent says 'another reason,' he must produce it (which he cannot); if he says 'the first,' he admits that the description can be absent, which undermines his objection. The author then argues that if something without a description—while it could remain devoid of it—necessitates a meaning, then something that cannot remain devoid of it is even more deserving of a meaning. Finally, he applies this to motion and rest: a substance cannot be moving or resting at the moment of origination, but thereafter it cannot remain devoid of both; thus motion or rest must occur, supporting the author's position.

Key Terms
الذات
الاتصاف
المعنى
الجوهر
العجز
الجهل
القادر
العالم
التعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof assumes the opponent already accepts that attributes require a meaning.
Correction: Rather, it uses a reductio showing the absurd consequences of denying it.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does the author highlight if the essence could necessitate an attribute in the second state without a new meaning?
A) The attribute would be impossible to acquire.
B) Incapacity would necessitate power, and ignorance would necessitate knowledge.
C) The substance would be devoid of all attributes.
D) The essence would change into something else.

Key Takeaway
The essence cannot necessitate an attribute without a meaning; otherwise, opposite attributes would be necessitated, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever attribute to Allah qualities that you have not carefully grounded in revelation? How does this logical principle—that attributes require a meaning in the essence—guard your understanding of Allah's names?
?
When you say 'Allah is Powerful' or 'Allah is Knowing,' do you truly reflect that these attributes are necessitated by His essence in a way that precludes change? How does this affect your trust in His eternal attributes?
?
If you were to accidentally fall into thinking that Allah's attributes could change or be added over time, how would this argument from Nasafi shake your soul? What would you need to reaffirm in your belief?

The Necessity of a Meaning for Attributes in Motion and Rest
Nasafi uses conditional arguments to prove necessity of meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how the impossibility of a substance remaining devoid of motion or rest further proves that attributes require a distinct meaning.

Core Idea
If a substance cannot remain devoid of a description, a meaning must occur to bring it about.

Building on the earlier arguments, Imam Nasafi presents two additional proofs that reinforce the necessity of a meaning. He argues that if a substance could remain devoid of a description, the occurrence of a meaning would be required; and since it cannot remain devoid in the case of motion and rest, a meaning must be affirmed. This lesson shows how these arguments align with the position of Ahl al-Tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage consists of two conditional arguments.
2
Understand the comparative argument
The first argument compares something that can remain devoid of a description with something that cannot. Identify the logical principle used.
The principle: if the former necessitates a meaning, the latter is even more deserving.
3
Apply the argument to motion and rest
The second argument applies the same principle to the specific case of motion and rest. Understand why the substance cannot remain devoid of both.
At origination it can be without motion/rest, but afterwards it must have one.

?

?

?

Refutation of the View That Permanence Is Inherent to the Essence
Imam Nasafi refutes the Karamiyya and al-Ṣaymarī claims about permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi uses linguistic and consensus arguments to refute the claim that permanence (baqa') is identical to existence.

Core Idea
Permanence is not identical to existence; language and consensus prove it is an additional attribute.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi responds to an objection from the Karamiyya sect and al-Ṣaymarī, who assert that a substance is described by permanence from the moment of its existence. Nasafi first notes that leading Muʿtazilites concede the premise, then refutes the objectors with three powerful linguistic arguments and a philosophical proof. Let's examine how he dismantles their position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening lines where the opponent argues that permanence is inherent to the substance from its first existence, just like existence itself.
This objection challenges the premise of Nasafi's earlier argument.
2
Distinguish between opponents
Notice how Nasafi separates the leading Mu'tazilites (who concede his premise) from the Karamiyya and al-Ṣaymarī, whom he will refute directly.
This shows his nuanced approach.
3
Analyze the first linguistic proof
Examine the argument that saying 'it existed and did not remain' is not contradictory, while 'it existed and did not exist' is. This shows baqa' is additional.
4
Analyze the second proof
Look at the supplication evidence: people say 'May God preserve you' but not 'May God create you'. If baqa' were existence, both would be acceptable.
5
Analyze the third proof and conclusion
Consider the synonymy of 'permanent' and 'eternal' and the consensus against calling contingents 'eternal'. Then note the beginning of the philosophical proof about necessity after origination.
The passage cuts off, but the direction is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا الاستدلال مبني على مقدمة مجحودة وهي: أنكم ادعيتم استحالة الوصف بالبقاء في حالة الوجود ، وهذا غير مسلم عند الكرامية فإنهم يزعمون أن الجوهر في أول أحوال وجوده موصوفا بالبقاء كما هو موصوف بالوجود، وإلى هذا القول يذهب عباد الصيمري صاحب هشام بن عمرو من جملة المعتزلة، كذا حكى عنه الكعبي في المقالات.
قلنا: إن المخالفين لنا في المسألة من رؤساء المعتزلة سلموا لنا هذا الذي بنينا فتكلمنا على ما مر معهم بناء على تسليمهم. فأما الكرامية والصيمري فإنا نقول لهم: هذا منكم جحد الضروريات وإنكار البداهة، وخرق إجماع أهل اللسان قاطبة، فإنهم يستجيزون أن يقولوا وجد كذا (ولم يبق ولو) كان البقاء راجعًا إلى الذات كالوجود لكان قول القائل وجد ولم يبق كقول القائل وجد ولم يوجد وحيث عد هذا تناقضا دون الأول، دل على بطلان هذا الرأي المستنزل المحدث. وكذا الناس يقولون في الدعاء : أبقاك الله، ولا يستجيز أحد أن يقول لآخر : أوجدك الله، ولو كان اللفظان في الحقيقة راجعين إلى شيء واحد لما امتنعوا عن إطلاق أحدهما الأطباق على إجازة إطلاق الآخر، وكذا الباقي والدائم من جملة الألفاظ المترادفة، ثم انعقد الإجماع على استحالة إطلاق لفظ الدائم فكذا الباقي. ويدل على صحة ما ذهبنا إليه وبطلان ما ذهب إليه الخصوم أن الباقي لو كان باقيًا لنفسه أو لا لمعنى أكثر من ذاته لكان وجوده واجبًا بعد حدوثه؛ لأن
For if it is said: This inference is based on a premise that is contested, namely that you claimed the impossibility of description by permanence (al-baqā’) in the state of existence, and this is not accepted by the Karamiyya, for they claim that the substance in the first of its states of existence is described by permanence just as it is described by existence; and ʿAbbād al-Ṣaymarī, the companion of Hishām ibn ʿAmr, among the Muʿtazila goes to this view; thus al-Kaʿbī narrated from him in the Maqālāt. We say: Those who oppose us on this issue among the leading Muʿtazila have conceded to us this premise upon which we built, so we spoke on what was mentioned with them based on their concession. As for the Karamiyya and al-Ṣaymarī, we say to them: This from you is a denial of necessary truths and a rejection of self-evident knowledge, and a breach of the consensus of all the people of language. For they allow themselves to say 'such and such existed (and did not remain, even if?)' If permanence were referable to the essence like existence, then the saying of one who says 'it existed and did not remain' would be like the saying of one who says 'it existed and did not exist'. And since this is counted a contradiction while the former is not, that indicates the invalidity of this newfangled opinion that has been contrived. Likewise, people say in supplication: 'May God preserve you (abqāka Allāh),' and no one allows saying to another: 'May God create you (awjadaka Allāh).' If the two words were in reality referable to one thing, they would not refrain unanimously from affirming one while permitting the other. Likewise, 'the permanent (al-bāqī)' and 'the eternal (al-dāʾim)' are among synonymous terms; then consensus has been reached on the impossibility of applying the term 'eternal' (al-dāʾim), so likewise 'the permanent' (al-bāqī). And it indicates the correctness of what we have gone to and the invalidity of what the opponents have gone to: that if the permanent were permanent by itself or without a meaning more than its essence, its existence would be necessary after its origination; because...
Source pages p. 799

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent objects that the previous argument assumes that permanence (baqā’) cannot be a description of a substance at the moment of origination, but the Karamiyya sect and ʿAbbād al-Ṣaymarī (a Muʿtazilī) claim that the substance is described by permanence from its first existence, just as it is described by existence. The author responds by first noting that the leading Muʿtazilites concede the premise (so the argument holds for them). He then refutes the Karamiyya and al-Ṣaymarī by accusing them of denying necessary truths and violating linguistic consensus. He gives three proofs: (1) People say 'it existed and did not remain' without contradiction, but 'it existed and did not exist' is a contradiction; this shows baqā’ is additional. (2) Supplications like 'May God preserve you' (abqāka Allāh) are used, but 'May God create you' (awjadaka Allāh) is not; if baqā’ were identical to existence, both would be permissible. (3) The terms 'permanent' (al-bāqī) and 'eternal' (al-dāʾim) are synonymous, and consensus forbids calling contingent beings 'eternal', so 'permanent' must also be additional. Finally, he begins a philosophical proof: if a contingent were permanent by itself, its existence would be necessary after origination (which is impossible). The argument is cut off at 'because...' due to the page ending.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam Nasafi rejects all Mu'tazilites in this argument.
Correction: He only refutes the Karamiyya and al-Ṣaymarī, while noting that leading Mu'tazilites actually concede his premise.

Checkpoint
What linguistic evidence does the author give to show that baqā’ is not identical to existence?
A) People say 'May God preserve you' but not 'May God create you'.
B) The terms 'permanent' and 'eternal' are synonyms, and consensus forbids calling contingent beings 'eternal'.
C) The phrase 'it existed and did not remain' is not contradictory, but 'it existed and did not exist' is.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi uses linguistic common sense and consensus to prove that permanence (baqa') is an additional attribute, distinct from existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very permanence each moment is not from yourself but a gift from Allah, how does that change the way you start your day? Will you remind yourself today that every breath you take is a new act of preservation, not a right you possess?
?
You often say 'May God preserve you' to others. Do you truly mean it as a plea that their existence continues only by Allah’s will, or is it just a polite phrase? How would your dua change if you realized that preservation is a distinct attribute that Allah could withdraw at any moment?
?
The author proves that permanence is additional to existence. If existence itself is a gift, and permanence is another, what does that say about your sense of entitlement? Is there something in your life you assume will always be there—faith, health, a loved one—that you are taking for granted as if it were self-sustaining? What will you actively do today to treat it as a loan from Allah?

6 min
Refuting the Identity of Baqa' and Essence
Refutation of Karramiyya and al-Saymari using linguistic arguments and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how linguistic usage and consensus demonstrate that baqa' is a distinct attribute from existence, not identical to the essence.

Core Idea
Language itself proves that 'remaining' is not the same as 'existing'.

The Karramiyya and al-Saymari claimed that baqa' is nothing but the essence itself. The author counters this by appealing to self-evident linguistic principles and consensus. Through two simple linguistic tests, he shows that baqa' must be an additional attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the shift from stating the opponent's view to the refutation.
2
Understand the first linguistic argument
The author argues that if baqa' were identical to existence, saying 'it existed and did not remain' would be as contradictory as 'it existed and did not exist'. Since the former is not contradictory, baqa' must be additional.
3
Understand the second argument and consensus
The second argument uses supplication: people say 'abqāka Allah' but not 'awjadaka Allah', showing a distinction. Then consensus that 'dā'im' cannot be used for God supports that 'bāqī' is also an attribute.
Both arguments rely on immediate linguistic intuition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حالة الوجود ، وهذا غير مسلم عند الكرامية فإنهم يزعمون أن الجوهر في أول أحوال وجوده موصوفا بالبقاء كما هو موصوف بالوجود، وإلى هذا القول يذهب عباد الصيمري صاحب هشام بن عمرو من جملة المعتزلة، كذا حكى عنه الكعبي في المقالات. قلنا: إن المخالفين لنا في المسألة من رؤساء المعتزلة سلموا لنا هذا الذي بنينا فتكلمنا على ما مر معهم بناء على تسليمهم. فأما الكرامية والصيمري فإنا نقول لهم: هذا منكم جحد الضروريات وإنكار البداهة، وخرق إجماع أهل اللسان قاطبة، فإنهم يستجيزون أن يقولوا وجد كذا ولم يبق ولو كان البقاء راجعًا إلى الذات كالوجود لكان قول القائل وجد ولم يبق كقول القائل وجد ولم يوجد وحيث عد هذا تناقضا دون الأول، دل على بطلان هذا الرأي المستنزل المحدث. وكذا الناس يقولون في الدعاء : أبقاك الله، ولا يستجيز أحد أن يقول لآخر : أوجدك الله، ولو كان اللفظان في الحقيقة راجعين إلى شيء واحد لما امتنعوا عن إطلاق أحدهما [؟] على إجازة إطلاق الآخر، وكذا الباقي والدائم من جملة الألفاظ المترادفة، ثم انعقد الإجماع على استحالة إطلاق لفظ الدائم فكذا الباقي.
The state of existence, and this is not conceded by the Karramiyya, for they claim that the substance in its first state of existence is described with remaining (al-baqā') just as it is described with existence. And to this opinion goes 'Abbād al-Ṣaymarī, the companion of Hishām ibn 'Amr, from among the Mu'tazila. Thus al-Ka'bī narrated from him in al-Maqālāt. We say: Indeed, those who oppose us in this issue from among the leaders of the Mu'tazila have conceded to us that upon which we built, so we spoke according to what passed with them based on their concession. As for the Karramiyya and al-Ṣaymarī, we say to them: This from you is denial of necessities and rejection of self-evident truths, and a breach of the consensus of all the people of language. For they permit saying 'It came into existence and did not remain' and if remaining were reducible to the essence like existence, then the statement of one who says 'It came into existence and did not remain' would be like the statement of one who says 'It came into existence and did not exist'. And since this is considered a contradiction without the former, it indicates the nullity of this fabricated and innovated opinion. Likewise, people say in supplication: 'May God make you remain (abqāka Allāh)', and no one permits saying to another 'May God bring you into existence (awjadaka Allāh)'. And if the two expressions in reality referred to one thing, they would not refrain from using one of them [؟] over the use of the other. Similarly, the enduring (al-bāqī) and the perpetual (al-dā'im) are among synonymous terms. Then the consensus has been established on the impossibility of using the term 'perpetual' for God. So likewise [it is impossible to use] 'enduring'.
Source pages p. 800

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim of the Karramiyya and al-Saymari that baqa' is identical to the essence. The author begins by stating the opponent's position: the Karramiyya and 'Abbād al-Ṣaymarī hold that a substance is described with baqa' from the very first moment of existence. The author then notes that some leading Mu'tazila conceded the premise that baqa' is distinct, so earlier arguments proceeded on that basis. But for the Karramiyya and al-Saymari, the author shifts to a more fundamental refutation: their view contradicts self-evident truths, necessities of language, and consensus. The first linguistic proof: if baqa' were reducible to the essence like existence, then saying 'it existed and did not remain' would be as contradictory as saying 'it existed and did not exist'. Since the former is not considered a contradiction, the claim fails. A second linguistic proof comes from supplicatory usage: people say 'May God make you remain' but never 'May God bring you into existence', indicating a distinction. Furthermore, the words bāqī and dā'im are synonyms, but consensus prohibits calling God dā'im; similarly, bāqī should be understood as an attribute, not the essence. Thus the opinion of the Karramiyya and al-Saymari is invalid.

بقاء (baqā')
جوهر (jawhar)
وجود (wujūd)
كرامية (Karramiyya)
صيمري (Ṣaymarī)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)
ضروريات (ḍarūriyyāt)
بداهة (badāhah)
إجماع (ijmā')
دائم (dā'im)
باق (bāqī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqa' is identical to the essence or existence.
Correction: Linguistic usage and consensus prove that baqa' is an additional attribute distinct from existence.

Checkpoint
What linguistic argument does the author use to show that baqa' is not the same as existence?
A) That the terms 'existence' and 'remaining' are never used together.
B) That saying 'it existed and did not remain' is not contradictory, unlike 'it existed and did not exist'.
C) That the word 'remaining' is never used in supplication.
D) That the Karramiyya themselves admit the distinction.

Key Takeaway
Baqa' is not synonymous with existence but is an additional attribute, as proven by self-evident linguistic facts and scholarly consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You regularly ask Allah to 'keep you firm' (thabbitni) but have you ever considered that 'keeping' (remaining) is distinct from mere existence? In what areas of your life do you assume God's blessings are automatic rather than actively sustained?
?
The Karramiyya denied the obvious linguistic distinction between existence and remaining. Are there any beliefs you hold that contradict clear evidence from revelation or reason? What would it take for you to abandon them?
?
The author uses the example of supplication: we say 'abqāka Allāh' but never 'awjadaka Allāh'. When you pray for someone to remain, do you truly recognize that God is the One who both creates and sustains? How does this affect your reliance on Him?
